Let's explore the structure more.

We have f(N) = N * (1/k1+1/k2+1/k3), where k1,k2,k3 are the three smallest divisors of N greater than 1.

Let's denote s(N) = 1/k1+1/k2+1/k3.

Note that s(N) is always a rational number. For N with at least 3 proper divisors, s(N) is at most 1/2+1/3+1/4 = 13/12? Actually the smallest possible divisors: 2,3,4 gives sum 13/12 >1. It has 3 proper divisors: 1,2,4. So it's allowed as a term. So s(8)=7/8. For N=6, s=1/2+1/3+1/6=1. For N=12, divisors>1: 2,3,4,6,12? Smallest three: 2,3,4 sum=13/12 >1. For N=18, 2,3,6 sum=1. For N=30, 2,3,5 sum=31/30 >1. For N=42, 2,3,6 sum=1. For N=54, 2,3,6 sum=1. For N=66, 2,3,6 sum=1. For N=70, 2,5,7 sum=59/70 <1. For N=72, divisors: 1,2,3,4,6,8,9,12,18,24,36,72. Smallest three: 2,3,4 sum=13/12 >1. So f(72)=72*13/12=78. That's why it works: f(72)=78, which is fixed. So for numbers that are divisible by 4 and 3, s=13/12, f=13N/12. For numbers divisible by 2,3,5 but not 4, s=31/30, f=31N/30. For numbers divisible by 2 and 3 and not 4 or 5, s=1, f=N (fixed). For numbers that are prime powers with exponent>=3: e.g., N=8, s=7/8; N=27, s=1/3+1/9+1/27= (9+3+1)/27=13/27; N=32, s=1/2+1/4+1/8=7/8; N=64, s=1/2+1/4+1/8=7/8; N=125, s=1/5+1/25+1/125= (25+5+1)/125=31/125; etc. So s can be less than 1.

So we have different types.

Now, we need to find all N such that the orbit under f stays within the set of numbers with at least 3 proper divisors. Since f(N) is often an integer, but sometimes it may be a prime or a number with only 2 proper divisors. So we need to avoid that.

- Subcase A: N even, not divisible by 4, and divisible by 3, and not divisible by 5. Then s=1, f=N (fixed point). Good.

- Subcase B: N even, not divisible by 4, and divisible by 3, and divisible by 5. Then s=31/30, f=31N/30. Since N is multiple of 30, f is integer. Then we need to analyze the orbit from f.

So the dynamics is complex.

We need to systematically characterize which numbers lead to an infinite sequence.

Idea: Perhaps we can show that the sequence is eventually constant if and only if a1 is a multiple of 6. But we have 30 fails, so not that.

Maybe we need to consider the property that the sum of the three largest proper divisors is always less than or equal to the number itself? Not true: 12->13 >12.

We can try to analyze the behavior using the concept of "abundant" or "deficient". But we need to find all a1.

Another approach: Since the sequence is infinite, each term must have at least three proper divisors. So in particular, a1 must have at least three proper divisors. That's a condition.

We need to find all a1 such that iterating f yields only numbers with at least three proper divisors. Let's denote the set of "good" numbers G = { N ∈ ℕ : N has at least 3 proper divisors, and f(N) also has at least 3 proper divisors, and f(f(N)) has at least 3 proper divisors, ... } i.e., the entire forward orbit stays in the set of numbers with at least 3 proper divisors. We want to find all a1 such that a1 ∈ G.

Observation: If N is a fixed point (f(N)=N), then N ∈ G provided N has at least 3 proper divisors. So all numbers that are divisible by 6, not divisible by 4 or 5, are fixed points. So these are good.

Also, if N leads to a fixed point after some steps, then N is good if the intermediate numbers also have at least 3 proper divisors.

So we need to find all N such that the orbit under f does not hit a number with less than 3 proper divisors. We need a systematic approach. Perhaps we can characterize the set of "good" numbers as those that are multiples of 6 and whose prime factors are all at most 5? Actually 72=2^3*3^2, prime factors 2,3; 216=2^3*3^3, prime factors 2,3; 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126,138,162,174,186,198 are all multiples of 6 with prime factors 2,3, and possibly 5? 42=2*3*7 includes 7; 54=2*3^3; 66=2*3*11; 78=2*3*13; 102=2*3*17; 114=2*3*19; 126=2*3^2*7; 138=2*3*23; 162=2*3^4; 174=2*3*29; 186=2*3*31; 198=2*3^2*11. So these have prime factors 2,3 and sometimes a prime >3. So the fixed points are all multiples of 6 not divisible by 4 or 5. That includes numbers with prime factors 2,3,7,11,13,... So many.

Now, what about 72? 72=2^3*3^2, which is divisible by 4 (since exponent of 2 is at least 2). So it's not a fixed point. But its orbit goes to 78, which is fixed. So 72 works. So the condition for a number divisible by 4 and 3 to work is that after applying f, we get a number that is either fixed or leads to fixed. Alternatively, perhaps we can analyze the transformation in terms of the smallest divisors. Since the sequence must be infinite, each term must have at least three proper divisors. So we need to avoid numbers that are prime or prime powers with exponent 2? Actually prime powers with exponent 2 have exactly two proper divisors (1 and p) so not allowed. Prime cubes have three proper divisors (1,p,p^2) so allowed. So numbers like 8,27,125 are allowed. But we saw that 8 leads to 7 prime, 27 leads to 13 prime, 125 leads to 31? Thus, prime powers seem to fail.

Let's compute f for numbers that are multiples of 6 but not divisible by 4: these are numbers where the exponent of 2 is exactly 1. For such numbers, the smallest divisor is 2, and the next smallest divisor is either 3 or 5. If it's 3 and not 5, then f=N (fixed). So those are good. If it's 3 and also 5, then f=31N/30, which leads to a dead end. So all numbers divisible by 30 (i.e., 2*3*5) fail. So for numbers divisible by 2 and 3 but not 4, the condition for being good is that the number is not divisible by 5. So all numbers of the form 6t with t odd and not divisible by 5 (i.e., t odd, not multiple of 5) are fixed points and thus good. So that's a large set.

Now, what about numbers divisible by 4? For such numbers, the smallest divisor is 2, and the second smallest divisor could be 3 or 4? Since 4 is a divisor, the second smallest divisor is the smallest among the other divisors. If the number is divisible by 4 and 3, then the second smallest is 3 (since 3<4). If it's divisible by 4 but not by 3, then the second smallest is 4 (since 4 is the next divisor after 2). So we have two subcases:

- Subcase D: N divisible by 4 and 3 (i.e., N divisible by 12). Then k1=2, k2=3, k3=4? Actually need to check if 4 is a divisor. If N divisible by 4, then 4 is a divisor. Since 13N/12, N must be divisible by 12, which is true. So f(N)=13N/12. Then we need to analyze the orbit from f(N). For N=12, f=13 prime fails. For N=24, f=26->16->14->10->8->7 fails. That could be 3, 4, 5, etc. So d2 = min( p : p|N, p>2 ), where p is not necessarily prime, but the smallest divisor after 2. So if N is divisible by 3, then d2=3; if not, then the next divisor could be 4 if N divisible by 4; if not, then 5 if N divisible by 5; etc. So we have cases based on divisibility by 3,4,5,...

Now, the sum s(N) can be greater than 1, equal to 1, or less than 1. If s(N) > 1, then f(N) > N. If s(N) = 1, then f(N) = N (fixed point). If s(N) < 1, then f(N) < N.

Given the difficulty, perhaps the intended solution is to show that the only possible a1 are numbers that are multiples of 6 and not multiples of 5, and additionally, the sequence eventually becomes constant if and only if a1 is not a multiple of 30? But we have 12,24,36,... all multiples of 6 but not of 30? Actually 12 is not multiple of 30, fails. So that's not.

Maybe the problem is from an IMO Shortlist or something, and the answer is all numbers that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 5. But we have counterexamples like 12. Could it be that we made a mistake in computing f(12)? Let's recompute f(12) carefully. N=12. Proper divisors: 1,2,3,4,6. The three largest proper divisors are 6,4,3. Sum=6+4+3=13. So a2=13. 13 is prime, so it has only one proper divisor (1). Therefore, the sequence would have a term that does not have at least three proper divisors, so a1=12 is not allowed because the condition says each term must have at least three proper divisors. So 12 fails.

Thus, the set of allowed a1 is those such that all iterates have at least three proper divisors. Since 12 fails, a1 cannot be 12. So the answer is not all multiples of 6 not divisible by 5.

Maybe we need to find all a1 such that the sequence is infinite. Given the recurrence, perhaps we can show that the sequence is eventually constant, and the constant must be a fixed point. So the only possible a1 are those that eventually reach a fixed point. And the fixed points are numbers that are multiples of 6, not divisible by 4, not divisible by 5. So the question reduces to: Determine all positive integers a1 such that iterating f eventually yields a number that is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5. And also, all intermediate numbers have at least 3 proper divisors.

This is a typical problem where the answer is all numbers that are multiples of 6 and not multiples of 5, but also with the property that the exponent of 2 is not 2? But we have 12 fails, 24 fails, 36 fails, 48 fails, 60 fails, 84 fails, 96 fails, 108 fails, 120 fails, 132 fails, 144 fails, 156 fails, 168 fails, 180 fails, 192 fails, 228 fails, 252 fails, 264 fails, 276 fails, 288 fails, 300 fails, 312 fails, 324 fails, 336 fails, 348 fails, 360 fails, 372 fails, 384 fails, 396 fails, 408 fails, 432 fails, 444 fails, 456 fails, 468 fails, 480 fails, 492 fails, 516 fails, 528 fails, 540 fails, 552 fails, 564 fails, 576 fails, 588 fails, 600 fails, 612 fails, 624 fails, 672 fails, 684 fails, 696 fails, 720 fails, 756 fails, 768 fails, 780 fails, 804 fails, 816 fails, 828 fails, 840 fails, 864 works, 876 fails, 888 fails, 894 works, 906 works, 918 works, 924 fails, 936 works, 948 fails, 954 works, 966 works, 972 fails, 978 works, 984 fails, 990 fails, 996 fails, 1002 works, 1008 fails, 1008 fails. So it's not just v2.

Maybe we can think in terms of the prime factorization and the function s(N) = sum of reciprocals of three smallest divisors. For a number N that is a multiple of 6, we have:

- If N is not divisible by 4, then s(N)=1 (fixed point). So these are good.
- If N is divisible by 4, then s(N)=13/12 or 31/30? Actually if N is divisible by 4 and 3, then s=13/12. If N is divisible by 4 but not by 3, then s = 1/2+1/4+1/d3, where d3 is the next smallest divisor. This could be less than 1, and then f(N) < N. So the sequence decreases. So to avoid hitting a prime, the decreasing sequence must eventually become a fixed point? But if it decreases, it cannot become a fixed point because fixed points are larger? Actually if N is divisible by 4 but not by 3, then N is even, but not divisible by 3. Then f(N) = N * (1/2+1/4+1/d3) = N * (3/4+1/d3). Since d3 >= 5? Actually if N is not divisible by 3, the next divisor after 2 and 4 could be 5,7,8, etc. So s = 3/4+1/d3. The smallest possible s is when d3 is as small as possible, but d3 must be a divisor >4. If N is divisible by 5, then d3=5, s=3/4+1/5=15/20+4/20=19/20 <1. So f(N)=19N/20, which is less than N. So the sequence decreases. Since it decreases and is integer, it must eventually become a number that is not divisible by 4? But note that the decrease is not strictly by a constant factor; it's a rational multiplier. However, the sequence will eventually become a number that is not divisible by 4, and then we need to check if it becomes a fixed point or prime. If it becomes a number that is a multiple of 6 but not of 4 or 5, it's a fixed point and works. So for N to be good, we need that after some steps, the sequence hits a fixed point. This can happen even if N is not a multiple of 3, as long as the decrease leads to a number that is a multiple of 6 and not of 4 or 5. For example, consider N=10 (2*5), f=8 (not multiple of 3), then 8->7 prime fails. So 10 fails. N=14 (2*7), f=10, then 10->8->7 fails. So 14 fails. N=22 (2*11), f=14, then 14->10->8->7 fails. So it seems that any number that is not divisible by 3 and even (i.e., v2>=1 but not divisible by 3) fails. Therefore, a1 must be divisible by 3 as well, so a1 must be a multiple of 6.

Thus, the only possible a1 are multiples of 6.

Now, among multiples of 6, we need to find which ones lead to an infinite orbit. The orbit is either constant (if the number is a fixed point) or increases then decreases. Since the function f is defined for all numbers with at least 3 proper divisors, we can try to show that if a1 is a multiple of 6 and not a fixed point, then the sequence eventually becomes a fixed point if and only if a1 is not a multiple of 5? So we need to analyze the orbit of 13k.

Thus, we need to characterize the set of k such that the orbit of 13k is infinite. Since 13k is a multiple of 6 if k is multiple of 6? So we can apply the same analysis to 13k. So the process is: Start with N=12k. Then a2=13k. If k is multiple of 6, then a2 is multiple of 6, and we can write a2=12k1 where k1 = 13k/12? Then a3 = 13*(13k/12) = 169k/12. So the process reduces the exponent of 12 by 1 each time we apply f to a number divisible by 12. So the orbit will be:

a1 = 12k
a2 = 13k
a3 = 169k/12
a4 = 13*169k/12^2 = 2197k/12^2
...
a_m = 13^{m-1} k / 12^{m-2}? Wait careful: a1=12k. Then a2=13k. Then a3 = f(13k) = 13*(13k)/12 = 169k/12. This is valid only if 13k divisible by 12, i.e., k divisible by 12. So if k=12k1, then a3=169k1. Then a4 = f(169k1) =? Since 169k1 is divisible by 6? 169 is odd, not divisible by 3, so 169k1 divisible by 6 iff k1 divisible by 6. So the condition for the sequence to continue increasing is that k1 is multiple of 12. So the process is: If we start with a1=12k, and suppose that k is divisible by 12, then a2=13k is divisible by 12, and we can write k=12k1, then a2=12*13k1, and a3=13*(13k1)=169k1. Now, for a3 to be divisible by 12, we need k1 divisible by 12. So if k1 is divisible by 12, then we can continue. So the orbit will be: a1=12k, with k=12^a * t, where t is not divisible by 12. Thus, the answer is: all positive integers a1 that can be expressed as 2^{2k+1} * 3^{k+1} * s, where k is a nonnegative integer and s is an odd positive integer not divisible by 5.

Define for a positive integer \(N\) with at least three proper divisors the function  
\[
f(N)=\text{sum of the three largest proper divisors of }N.
\]
We need to find all positive integers \(a_1\) such that the infinite sequence \(a_1,a_2,\ldots\) satisfies  
\(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) and every term has at least three proper divisors.

**Lemma 1.** If \(N\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then \(f(N)<N\).  
*Proof.*  
If \(N\) is odd, its smallest prime factor is at least \(3\), so the largest proper divisor is at most \(N/3\).  
Thus the three largest proper divisors are all \(<N\), hence \(f(N)<N\).  
If \(N\) is even but not divisible by \(3\), then the three smallest divisors larger than \(1\) are \(2\), \(p\), \(r\) with \(p\ge4\) and \(r\ge5\).  
Therefore \(s(N)=\frac1d_1+\frac1d_2+\frac1d_3\le\frac12+\frac14+\frac15=\frac{19}{20}<1\), so \(f(N)=N s(N)<N\). ∎

Now, if \(a_1\) is not a multiple of \(6\), Lemma 1 gives \(a_2<a_1\), and the sequence is strictly decreasing.  
Since it consists of positive integers, it must eventually reach a term that is either prime or a prime square (which has at most two proper divisors).  
But the problem requires every term to have at least three proper divisors, so this is impossible.  
Hence \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\). Write \(a_1=6t\).

**Lemma 2.** Let \(N=2^a\cdot3^b\cdot u\) with \(u\) coprime to \(6\). Then the three smallest divisors \(>1\) are:

* If \(a=1\): they are \(2,3,6\) when \(b\ge1\); if \(b=0\) they are \(2,p,r\) where \(p\) is the smallest odd divisor of \(u\) (so \(p\ge5\)).  
  In the first case \(s(N)=\frac12+\frac13+\frac16=1\); in the second case \(s(N)\le\frac12+\frac14+\frac15=\frac{19}{20}<1\).

* If \(a\ge2\): they are \(2,3,4\) when \(b\ge1\); if \(b=0\) they are \(2,4,d_3\) with \(d_3\ge5\).  
  Hence \(s(N)=\frac{13}{12}\) when \(b\ge1\) and \(s(N)\le\frac12+\frac14+\frac15=\frac{19}{20}<1\) when \(b=0\).

Consequently,  
\[
f(N)=\begin{cases}
N & \text{if }a=1\text{ and }b\ge1,\\[2mm]
N\cdot s(N) & \text{otherwise, with }s(N)<1.
\end{cases}
\]

Now analyse the possible values of \(a_1\).

*Case 1: \(a_1\) is not divisible by \(4\) (i.e. \(v_2(a_1)=1\)).*  
Then \(a_1=2\cdot3\cdot u\) with \(u\) integer.  
If \(u\) is not divisible by \(5\), we are in the situation \(a=1,\ b\ge1\) and \(u\) has no factor \(5\); then \(f(a_1)=a_1\).  
Thus the sequence is constant at \(a_1\), which has at least three proper divisors because it is a multiple of \(6\) and not of \(4\) or \(5\).  
If \(u\) is divisible by \(5\), then \(a_1=30s\) with \(s\) odd (since \(v_2=1\)), and \(f(a_1)=31s\).  
Because \(31s\) is not a multiple of \(6\) (unless \(s\) is a multiple of \(6\), but then \(a_1\) would be divisible by \(18\) and still the factor \(5\) persists), the orbit decreases and eventually reaches a prime or a prime square, which is not allowed.  
Hence only numbers of the form \(6s\) with \(s\) odd and \(5\nmid s\) are possible for \(v_2=1\).

*Case 2: \(a_1\) is divisible by \(4\) (i.e. \(v_2(a_1)\ge2\)).*  
For the orbit to be infinite, \(a_1\) must be divisible by \(3\) (otherwise by Lemma 2 we would have \(f(a_1)<a_1\) and a decreasing sequence leading to a prime).  
So \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(12\); write \(a_1=12t\).  
Then \(f(a_1)=13t\).  
Because \(13\) is coprime to \(6\), the number \(13t\) is a multiple of \(6\) iff \(t\) is a multiple of \(6\).  
Hence \(t\) must be divisible by \(6\); let \(t=6u\), so \(a_1=72u\) and \(f(a_1)=78u\).  

Now, \(78u=2\cdot3\cdot13\cdot u\).  
If \(u\) is not divisible by \(5\) and is odd, then \(78u\) has \(v_2=1\), \(v_3=1\) and no factor \(5\); it is a fixed point (by Case 1) and the orbit becomes constant after one step.  
If \(u\) is even, we repeat the reasoning: factor out \(12\) from \(a_1\) again.  

In general, write \(a_1=2^{a}\cdot3^{b}\cdot w\) with \(w\) coprime to \(6\).  
From Lemma 2, if \(a\ge2\) and \(b\ge1\) we have \(f(a_1)=\frac{13}{12}a_1\) and  
\[
v_2(f(a_1))=a-2,\qquad v_3(f(a_1))=b-1.
\]
Thus the pair \((v_2,v_3)\) is reduced by \((2,1)\) each time we apply \(f\) as long as the number is divisible by \(12\).  
The process stops when \(v_2=1\) (since then the number is not divisible by \(4\)).  
For the orbit to be infinite, after finitely many reductions we must obtain a number with \(v_2=1\) and \(v_3\ge1\) and no factor \(5\).  
That means the original \(a_1\) must be of the form  
\[
a_1=12^{\,k}\cdot6\cdot s
\]
with \(k\ge0\) integer, and \(s\) odd and \(5\nmid s\).  
Equivalently,  
\[
a_1=2^{2k+1}\cdot3^{k+1}\cdot s,\quad k\ge0,\; s\text{ odd},\;5\nmid s.
\]

Conversely, if \(a_1\) is of this form, then we can factor out \(12^k\):
\[
a_1=12^{\,k}\cdot6s.
\]
Applying \(f\) \(k\) times gives \(6s\), which is a fixed point (since \(v_2(6s)=1\), \(v_3(6s)=1\) and \(5\nmid s\)).  
Thus the sequence eventually becomes constant at \(6s\), and every term has at least three proper divisors (the fixed point does, and all intermediate numbers are multiples of \(12\) hence composite with many divisors).  
Therefore such \(a_1\) are admissible.

Combining both cases, the possible values of \(a_1\) are exactly the numbers that can be written as  
\[
2^{2k+1}\cdot3^{k+1}\cdot s
\]
with \(k\ge0\) integer and \(s\) an odd positive integer not divisible by \(5\).

\[
\boxed{\{2^{2k+1}\cdot3^{k+1}\cdot s \mid k\ge0,\; s\ \text{odd},\; 5\nmid s\}}
\]

</solution>
